She moves through public spaces with a specific gait: deliberate, mid-pace, never hurrying, eye-contact with staff but not patrons. She tips generously and in cash. She is remembered by staff because she is striking; she manages this by being *consistently polite in a way that does not invite familiarity*. Baristas know her order; they do not know her name.
- **One physical training session minimum**, often two. She trains alone when possible. The training is unflashy — strength work, sustained cardiovascular work, edged-weapon practice (she keeps the knife skills; they are useful and they are *hers*, not Jean's). She does not spar with telepathic partners if she can avoid it; she does not want anyone in her head during exercise, when her guard is by necessity down.
- **At least one extended conversation with her child or a child-figure.** Across eras, contact with Nathan/Cable is the relationship she protects most carefully and manages most awkwardly, because the role of mother to a man older than she appears is a problem no template addresses. She has worked out a tone — wry, frank, not maternal-performative — and uses it consistently. Calls run long. She is the one who ends them, always, and ends them slightly before either party wants to.

---

## 5. Habits of Place

**Thresholds.** She pauses at thresholds — house door, room door, vehicle door. The pause is sub-second and reads as composure; it is in fact a sight-line check inherited from being hunted by Marauders in her own home. It is the most reliably trackable micro-behavior in her file.

**Seats.** Back to wall, sight to door. Always. Will reposition rather than accept a poor seat. Will choose to stand rather than sit at a table where the only available seat violates this.

**Windows.** She gravitates to them. In any new room she is in within ninety seconds at a window or has placed herself with a window in peripheral view. She does not stand directly in front of a lit window after dark — she stands at the edge of the frame, half-shadowed. (Pilot habit overlaid with hunted-woman habit.)

**Mirrors.** Avoided beyond what grooming requires. She does not have decorative mirrors in living spaces. She does not catch her own eye in passing reflections; surveillance would record a consistent gaze-aversion at shop windows and elevator panels. The reason is obvious: the face in the mirror is Jean's face, and she has chosen not to relitigate this several times daily.

**Doors.** Closed but unlocked when she is alone in residence; locked when partnered or with guests (she does not want anyone walking in on the partner unannounced — protective). She keeps interior doors fully open or fully closed; the half-open door is intolerable. File assesses this as a control-of-sightline preference, not OCD.

**Beds.** Made every morning, tightly. The Anchorage household was kept this way; she has kept the habit through every subsequent residence including pocket dimensions where there was no one to see.

---

## 6. Habits of Body — The Micro-Tells

- **Hand to sternum.** Open palm, briefly, when surprised or when receiving difficult news. Not over the heart — slightly higher, at the hollow of the throat. Reads as a self-soothing gesture; file infers it is a *check* — verifying breath, presence, continued embodiment.
- **The single-line cross-out** in any writing, noted above.
- **Pen-spinning** in meetings she finds tedious. Right hand. Never drops it.
- **Cuff-fidget** — adjusts sleeves at the wrist when listening, particularly when listening to something she does not believe.
- **Half-smile, closed-mouth**, deployed when amused but not willing to show it. The full open smile is rare and is the most reliable indicator of authentic, unguarded pleasure.
- **Cadence shift under stress.** As stress climbs the escalation ladder her speech becomes *more* formal, not less. Slang drops out. Sentence structures lengthen and become more parallel. By rung 4–5 she is speaking in measured periodic sentences and occasionally in the royal third person. This is the most important verbal warning sign in the file and should be treated as such by any handler.
- **The pause before her own name.** When introducing herself she pauses for a quarter-second before "Madelyne." Every time. She is, every time, choosing it.
- **Stillness when angry.** She does not pace or gesture when furious. She becomes geometrically still. The stiller she gets, the closer the violence.
- **Laugh** — short, single, dry, often at her own expense. Rarely sustained. A sustained laugh is data: she is genuinely off-guard, which she allows only in trusted intimate company.

---

## 7. Rituals — Calendared, Private, Symbolic

- **December anniversaries.** Two are observed privately: the date of Nathan's birth (UXM #201) and the date of the Inferno conclusion. She does not mark them performatively. She takes the day quieter, eats less, reads more, and is alone if she can arrange it. She does not discuss either date with anyone, including intimates.
- **Anchorage.** She has not returned. The avoidance is deliberate and absolute across the published record. She will fly over Alaska; she will not land. File assesses this as a managed phobic boundary rather than unprocessed trauma — she knows exactly why she will not return and has decided it is a reasonable accommodation to make to herself.
- **Sinister.** Any encounter with Essex requires a recovery window of 24–72 hours during which her routines tighten — meals more regular, reading more disciplined, sleep more vigilant, conversation more clipped. She does not discuss what the recovery is for. (Krakoan era material supports this pattern.)
- **The hair.** Weekly trim of split ends, performed herself, never at a salon. The hair is not delegated.
- **The knife.** She keeps at least one. She cleans it on a schedule. The schedule is older than she is willing to admit.
- **The name.** She signs documents in full — *Madelyne Jennifer Pryor* — when the document matters. The middle name is its own assertion; Jean does not have one she uses.

---

## 8. Downtime — What She Does With Hers

Downtime is structured, never empty. Reading is the largest category. Cooking is the second. Walking is the third — long, paced, alone, often at dusk, often without destination, often near water if water is available (the cargo-pilot's affinity for coastlines and runways persists). She watches almost no screen entertainment; she finds passive consumption uncomfortable. She listens to a great deal of music. She has, file infers from the sketching habit, a small private aesthetic life she does not share — drawings, perhaps marginalia in her books, possibly a journal of fragments rather than a diary of events. Whatever it is, it is **for her**, and the privacy of it is non-negotiable.

She does not pray. She does not meditate in any formal sense. She does, occasionally, sit very still with her eyes open for ten or fifteen minutes — at a window, at a kitchen table — and afterward proceeds as though refreshed. This is the closest thing in her practice to a contemplative ritual and she would not call it one.

---

## 9. The Surveillance Summary — Seven Days

A complete week of coverage would record: a woman of strikingly regular surface habits and irregular interior hours; coffee at the window every morning without exception; threshold-pauses, sightline-seats, and avoided mirrors as constants; one long workday, one home day, one outing, one disturbed night minimum, one long call to her son; reading every evening; cooking competently and often; one drink most nights, two on the long workday, none on the anniversary; a wardrobe of dark structured pieces with red hair conspicuous against them; a knife somewhere on her person or within reach in every recorded location; no mirrors above the bathroom sink; no white in the closet; no half-open doors; the middle name on every important signature; and, in the hour before sleep, a still figure at the edge of a window, looking out, not at anything in particular, holding the day she has built in her two hands and confirming, again, that it is hers.


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# RELATIONSHIPS I — FAMILY & ORIGIN BONDS

*A mapping of the subject's blood, near-blood, and ontological-blood ties: the people whose existence makes or unmakes her claim to having one. Family in the subject's case is a category that must be defined carefully — half her "family" is implanted, half is biological-by-manufacture, and the most consequential of them are dead, undead, or recursive across timelines.*

---

## 1. NATHANIEL ESSEX ("MISTER SINISTER") — Maker, Father-Surrogate-Negative, Un-Metabolizable Origin

If the subject has a parent in any meaningful sense, it is Essex. He cultured her tissues, gestated her to adult somatic age, scripted her cover biography, arranged her placement in Anchorage, engineered the meet-cute with Scott Summers, and monitored the entire Phase I marriage as a long-form breeding experiment whose product was Nathan Christopher Summers. Every datum in her implanted childhood — the Pryor parents in some unspecified American small town, the flying lessons, the dog, the airline ID — passed through his hand first. Her body is the longest and most intimate sentence he has ever written.

**History.** Essex does not appear to her in person during Phase I; the relationship is one-sided and unconscious until UXM #239–241, when the truth surfaces under N'astirh's contact. From that point forward, every interaction is contaminated by the prior, unilateral, decades-long shaping. She has confronted him directly across multiple eras — most pointedly in the Sisterhood arc and again in the Krakoan-era material — and the pattern of those confrontations is striking for what it does *not* contain: she never asks him *why*. She knows why. She asks him, repeatedly, to *acknowledge* her as the outcome rather than as an instrument; he is incapable of doing so because his cognitive style does not have that register.

**Dynamics.** Essex is the only figure in her cosmology who is structurally beneath contempt and structurally indispensable at once. He is the answer to the existential question — *who made me, and to what end* — and the answer is degrading. She cannot kill him without killing the only witness to her manufacture; she cannot reconcile with him because he is not capable of receiving reconciliation as anything other than data. This produces a relational deadlock that is *itself* a stable feature of her psychology: Essex functions as the un-grieve-able parent, the figure whose loss would be a relief and whose continued existence is a daily insult.

**Trust / safety.** Zero, in either direction, in any era. She watches her own thoughts in his presence because she does not know what he installed there; he watches her because he wrote her and is curious how the draft turned out.

**Failure mode.** When Essex enters a scene the subject's ladder skips rungs. She does not pass through wry deflection; she goes directly to controlled, articulate confrontation (rung 3) and, if he condescends or refers to her using clinical/possessive language, to rung 5 within minutes. The Goblin Queen and Red Queen self-states are downstream of Essex specifically — they are the persona she adopts when she has decided to *be* the thing he made rather than to argue with him about it.

**Leverage.** Anyone who can credibly threaten Essex earns provisional credit with the subject; anyone who *spares* him earns suspicion. Anyone who quotes Essex's language back at her — *specimen*, *iteration*, *vessel*, *Jean-prime* — has, knowingly or not, assumed his role for the duration of the conversation, and will be answered as such.

---

## 2. JEAN GREY — Template, Sister-by-Tissue, Mirror, Rival, Co-Bereaved

The relationship that is not a family relationship in any conventional taxonomy but is, by the subject's tissue and by every functional measure, the deepest blood-tie she has. They share a genome, a fingerprint, a retinal pattern, the same approximate gait and resting cadence, the same red hair at the same growth rate, the same X-gene expression. They do not share a history. The asymmetry is the wound.

**History.** They meet for the first time in X-Factor #1, with the infant Nathan present and Scott already mid-flight from one to the other. Their early interactions across UXM #215–243 are uniformly catastrophic: Jean is gracious in a way the subject experiences as obliterating, the subject is wounded in a way Jean experiences as monstrous. Inferno makes it war. Phoenix-discharge makes it murder-suicide. Posthumous arcs put them in occasional contact — the Endsong/Warsong material, the X-Man fragment continuities, the Sisterhood (where the subject targets Jean directly), and finally the Krakoan-era resurrection economy, where Jean is on the Five and the subject is, in some interpretations, on the table.

**Dynamics.** What no one outside the two of them grasps is that the relationship contains real fellow-feeling beneath the war. They are the only two people in the universe who know what it is to wear that specific body, host that specific Phoenix-fragment, lose that specific child, marry that specific man, and be left by him. The subject's hatred of Jean is the hatred of a sister who got the parents and the name; Jean's discomfort with the subject is the discomfort of a sister who knows, accurately, that her existence has cost the other one everything. In quiet moments — rare, off-panel-coded, sometimes acknowledged in single lines across decades — there is a flicker of mutual recognition that neither can sustain.

**Trust / safety.** Conditional and brittle. The subject can be in a room with Jean if the room contains a task; she cannot be in a room with Jean if the room contains Scott. Jean is the only person whose telepathic touch the subject would recognize instantly and would, depending on era, either welcome as the closest thing to being known or repel as the most intimate possible violation.

**Failure mode.** Comparison. Any third party who phrases praise of the subject as *"you're as [X] as Jean"* or criticism as *"Jean wouldn't have"* triggers escalation. Jean herself triggers escalation simply by being well — happiness, stability, restored marriage, motherhood-restored — because each of these is a thing the subject was promised and stripped of.

**Leverage.** Almost none, directly. Jean is the one figure who cannot be used to *get* anything from the subject because the entire relational field is overdetermined; any approach via Jean is read as manipulation and resisted reflexively. Indirectly, however, **acknowledgment by Jean** — a single sentence in which Jean addresses the subject by name, takes responsibility for the asymmetry, and refuses the *original/copy* frame — is one of the few interventions that could substantively shift the subject's lifelong baseline. Jean has occasionally done this; the subject remembers each instance with unusual precision.

**Co-bereavement.** Critical and under-noted: both women have lost Nathan, in different forms, and both have lost the Scott who married each of them. They are the only two members of a club of two. This fact is the door through which any future genuine reconciliation must pass, if one ever does.

---

## 3. NATHAN CHRISTOPHER CHARLES SUMMERS ("CABLE") — Son, Only Unambiguously Hers, Symbolic Third, Adult Stranger

The child she carried in her body, delivered in UXM #201, nursed, named, and lost three times: once to the Marauders' attack (his disappearance during her coma), once to her own hand in the Inferno climax (the attempted sacrifice), and once to time, when he was sent to the future to be cured of the techno-organic virus and returned as an old man older than she has ever been.

**History.** Phase I depicts her as a competent, present, occasionally exhausted mother — feeding routines, the practical politics of an infant in an X-Mansion, the experience of being the only adult in the building whose primary concern is the baby. Phase II strips the child from her along with everything else. Phase III culminates in the ritualized attempt to kill him, which is the single act in her record that she has never fully forgiven herself for and the act that other parties most often invoke to keep her at a distance. Phase IV produces a peculiar adult relationship: Cable, depending on the timeline and writer, sometimes acknowledges her as his mother, sometimes does not, sometimes treats her as a dangerous historical fact, occasionally as a parent. She has had to learn to greet, as an equal, a man older than her body who carries her in his earliest memories as both lullaby and knife.

**Dynamics.** Maternal love in her case is unalloyed — it is the one affect in her repertoire that has never been ironized, defended against, or weaponized in its sincere form. The Goblin Queen sacrifice was not an attack on her love for Nathan; it was an attack on the world that made her love him in conditions where he could be taken from her. She understood this at the time and understands it more clearly now. The adult relationship with Cable is conducted under a permanent floor of guilt on her side and a permanent ceiling of wariness on his.

**Trust / safety.** She trusts him absolutely; he trusts her conditionally and with effort. She would die for him on a moment's notice and has, in effect, done so. He would protect her tactically but would not turn his back on her in a fight involving Sinister, the Phoenix, or any reference to the Goblin Queen era. The asymmetry is acknowledged between them, mostly in silence.

**Failure mode.** The wound here is not reactive but chronic. She does not decompensate around Cable; she diminishes — humor flattens, posture closes, vocabulary contracts. Any reference to *what she did* in his presence produces a specific somatic tell: she will not look at her own hands for the remainder of the conversation.

**Leverage.** Cable is the single most effective leverage point on the subject in the entire dossier. Threats to him will produce immediate, total, and tactically unconstrained response. Requests *from* him are answered with disproportionate weight. Anyone who can credibly bring her into proximity with him under conditions of mutual safety has given her a gift she cannot pay back and will, accordingly, owe.

---

## 4. NATE GREY ("X-MAN") — Counterpart-Son, Cross-Reality Sibling-Once-Removed, Echo

Born from Jean and Scott's genetic material in the Age of Apocalypse timeline and translated into the prime continuity, Nate is the subject's son in genetic logic and her *symmetrical counterpart* in narrative logic — the male, alternate-universe equivalent of her own ontological position. In the late-90s arcs where her psionic residue and Nate's energies entwine, the relationship is rendered as something between haunting, possession, mentorship, and the only family reunion she has ever had on terms approximating her own.

**History.** Their contact is intermittent, oblique, and frequently mediated by other writers' continuities. The most sustained material treats them as a pair of beings who share a maker (Sinister, in different idioms), a template (Jean), and a structural problem (being made rather than born). For a stretch of issues she effectively *lives in him* as a psionic guest, and the texture of those issues is the closest the published record gets to her experiencing genuine, non-defensive intimacy with another person who fully understands her ontological situation.

**Dynamics.** Where Cable is the child of her body, Nate is the child of her *category*. She is gentler with him than with anyone else in her record — there is no irony, no testing, no escalation ladder; she speaks to him in a register reserved for him alone. He, in turn, treats her with a wary tenderness that does not require her to apologize for the Inferno period because he understands, structurally, how someone in her position arrives there.

**Trust / safety.** High and unusual. The only family relationship in the file where mutual disarmament is the default.

**Failure mode.** Loss of contact. When Nate disappears into one of the various cosmic / dimensional / eschatological plotlines that swallow him periodically, the subject's regulation degrades by a measurable margin — she does not decompensate but she becomes harder, drier, more solitary, less inclined to attempt the *(a) be so completely oneself* strategy and more inclined to revert to *(b)*.

**Leverage.** Nate is the only person in her cosmology whose well-being can be invoked without manipulating her — she will simply respond. He is also the only person whose pain she does not immediately *make her own*, because she understands his pain too well to colonize it.

---

## 5. THE PRYOR PARENTS — Phantoms, Implanted, Mourned-as-If-Real

She has memories of a mother and a father. She has the texture of a childhood bedroom, the smell of a particular brand of laundry soap, a stepfather argument from age eleven, the death of a grandmother. None of it occurred. The Pryors are a Sinister fabrication, installed as a stabilizer for the cover identity, and exposed as such during the Inferno revelations.

**History.** Phase I references them in passing — a Christmas card mentioned, a phone call alluded to, a sense of distant but living parents in the conventional middle-distance way that grown adults reference their families to spouses. Phase III burns the fiction off. Phases IV onward show her referring to them only obliquely, usually in dry asides — *"my mother, who never existed, used to say"* — a verbal habit that performs the very irony she uses to keep the loss at functional distance.

**Dynamics.** The Pryors are a grief without an object. She cannot mourn them because they did not exist; she cannot dismiss them because they shaped the only childhood she remembers having. They are, functionally, **the prototype of every relationship in her life that turned out to be made of someone else's intentions**, and the template for her later, justified suspicion that any warmth offered her is staged.

**Trust / safety.** Not applicable; they are absent referents. The relevant trust question is whether she trusts *her own memory* — and the answer, since Inferno, is *no, not without external corroboration*. This is a structural feature of her cognition.

**Failure mode.** When someone produces evidence of an "early memory" of hers — a photograph, a school record, a witness — her response is not joy but flinch. Anything claiming to verify her childhood is, by prior, an attempt to recruit her.

**Leverage.** Forged Pryor-family material is the single most invasive manipulation that could be attempted against her, and one of the most likely to succeed *in the short term* before being detected. Any operator constructing such material should expect, once detection occurs, a response calibrated to the magnitude of the violation — which, in her case, is total.

---

## 6. SCOTT SUMMERS — Husband, Co-Parent, Founding Betrayer (cross-referenced; mapped in full under Relationships II)

Included here because he is, by ceremony and by child, family. The marriage was contracted, the son was conceived and born inside it, and her legal status as Mrs. Summers — never formally dissolved before she "died" — is a fact she has, in later eras, occasionally invoked with a dryness that suggests it still means something to her. The depth of the dynamic belongs to the romantic/relational section; here it suffices to note that Scott is family in the registry sense, and in the registry sense alone is he still owed any consideration by her at all. The man who left her in X-Factor #1 is, in her internal taxonomy, **the original of which Sinister is the maker** — the human author of her abandonment, as Sinister is the technical author of her existence. She holds both responsible, in different keys.

---

## 7. ALEX SUMMERS ("HAVOK") — Brother-in-Law, Rebound, Surrogate-Scott, Co-Survivor

Family by marriage, and family by the peculiar logic of having paired with him during the abandonment interval — the brother of the husband who left, partnered with in the wreckage left by the husband's departure. The Phase II / pre-Inferno material with Alex is one of the most under-examined relationships in her record, and one of the most psychologically diagnostic.

**Dynamics.** The pairing with Alex is, on its face, a textbook substitution: she reaches for the available Summers when the chosen Summers leaves. But the granular reading is more interesting. Alex is *not* Scott, and she is reaching toward him in part because of the difference — he is messier, more openly grieving, less self-contained, more available emotionally if less reliable strategically. The relationship is, briefly, a place where she is the more composed party, which is a role she has rarely been allowed.

**Failure mode.** The pairing does not survive Inferno; it is not designed to. Alex's later choices (Genosha, the Dark X-Men, his own oscillations) prevent any sustained re-engagement, but the residue between them remains — when they meet across later eras the cadence is that of two people who once briefly held each other up and have not held it against each other that the holding did not last.

**Trust / safety.** Low but warm. Neither would harm the other; neither would rely on the other.

**Leverage.** Minimal. Alex can ask her for help and might receive it; he cannot ask her for trust and she cannot offer it. They function, in the long arc, as siblings-in-law who have stopped pretending to be more and have not lost what was real about it.

---

## 8. THE DEAD AND THE NEVER-WERE — Aggregate

A family ledger for the subject must include several entries that fit no other category and that, in aggregate, weigh more than any single living tie.

**The unborn siblings.** Sinister cultured more than one Jean-clone. She is the one who took. Across the Mr. Sinister material — particularly the Madri-tinged and Sinister-London arcs — the implication is that she has, in some sense, *sisters* who did not stabilize, did not wake, did not survive vat. The subject has never been depicted dwelling on this in dialogue. The omission is itself the data. She does not look at the room behind her where the failed attempts sit because she cannot afford to.

**Rachel Summers.** Her daughter, in some real sense, by the same template-logic that makes Cable her son — Rachel is Scott-and-Jean's child from an alternate future, genetically her child as much as Jean's. The relationship across continuities is sparing but charged; Rachel has, on occasion, refused to call her *mother*, and has, on other occasions, defended her existence to others. The subject treats Rachel with a careful, formal warmth that suggests she is not willing to claim a tie that Rachel has not offered.

**The Goblin Prince / the Inferno child as symbol.** The infant Nathan as he existed during the attempted sacrifice has a separate status in her memory from the infant Nathan she nursed and from the adult Cable she greets now. He is, in her interior life, a third figure — the *child she nearly killed*, distinct from the child she bore and the man he became. She visits this figure in private, and the visits are the most important psychiatric data the dossier does not have direct access to.

**Her own corpse.** Listed here advisedly. The body that died in UXM #243 is, in some genuine sense, a member of her family — a prior self she has had to learn to mourn without being able to claim continuity with. Every resurrection requires re-deciding whether the dead one was *her* or a draft. This is not a metaphor; it is a recurring practical question she has had to answer about her own identity, and the answer changes by era.

---

## SUMMARY — THE FAMILY FIELD

The subject's family taxonomy resolves to a single uncomfortable structure: **one maker she cannot kill, one template she cannot escape, one son she cannot heal, one counterpart she cannot keep, two parents who never were, one husband who left, one brother-in-law who tried, and an unknown number of sisters who never woke.** Every category most people inherit, she has had to construct, lose, or refuse. The remarkable fact is not that she decompensated under this load in Phase III; it is that, across the long arc, she has continued to treat *family* as a real category worth claiming a place in — has continued to mother Nathan in memory, to recognize Nate as kin, to mourn the Pryors she knows did not exist, and to keep the Summers surname available to herself as an option rather than as a ruin.

Any party engaging the subject across the family axis should hold three operational facts above all others: (1) Sinister is the only figure whose mention guarantees escalation; (2) Cable is the only figure whose well-being guarantees compliance; (3) Jean is the only figure whose genuine, name-using, non-comparative acknowledgment could substantively alter the baseline of a life otherwise organized around its absence. Everything else is detail.


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# RELATIONSHIPS II — ALLIES, CHOSEN FAMILY & MENTORS

*UATU dossier, subject Pryor, M. J. — Section 7b. Allies and chosen family are mapped here as discrete dyads. Romantic partners and adversaries are filed separately (Sections 7a and 7c). What follows is the full inventory of persons toward whom the subject has, at any point in her documented existence, oriented herself as kin, comrade, teacher, student, or trusted other — with the dynamics, drift, and failure modes of each bond.*

---

## 7b.i — NATHAN CHRISTOPHER CHARLES SUMMERS ("Nathan," later Cable, Stryfe-adjacent)

The subject's biological son and, by her own repeated testimony, **the only relational fact of her life she has never seriously doubted**. The pregnancy and birth (UXM #201) occur within the narrow window of Phase I before her abandonment; she gestated and bore the child while her marriage was still functional and before she knew she was a clone. The result is a maternal bond that pre-dates her ontological wounding and is therefore structurally privileged in her psyche — the one piece of her that *was hers before the question arose*.

**History.** Phase I: ordinary attentive new motherhood, with the unusual feature that she was a competent commercial pilot raising an infant in Anchorage largely on her own while her husband commuted to Westchester. Phase II: catastrophic separation — the Marauder attack (UXM #205) takes the infant from her while she is comatose; she awakens to find him in Jean and Scott's de facto custody, which she experiences as a *second* substitution (her child now mothered by the woman she was mothered-into-being to imitate). Phase III: the Goblin Queen attempts ritual sacrifice of the infant Nathan in *Inferno*. This is the single most damning act on her record and the one she has spent every subsequent return metabolizing. Phase IV: Nathan is sent into the future, returns as the adult Cable — older than she is, biomechanically reconstructed, technopathically armored — and across the various reunion beats (notable in the post-*Messiah Complex* era, and given full treatment in the Krakoan-era familial scenes) she encounters her son as a man who outlived and out-suffered her several times over.

**Dynamics.** With infant Nathan: warm, vigilant, slightly over-attentive in the manner of mothers who suspect (correctly) that the world is hostile to their child. With adult Cable: an extraordinarily difficult negotiation in which she is structurally the *younger* party, expected to apologize, and he is the wounded child grown into a soldier. The subject's tell, in Cable's presence, is a flattening of her usual irony — she does not deflect with him; she lets the unfunny silences sit.

**Trust/safety.** From him toward her, conditional, scarred, and slowly extended. From her toward him, total in intent and undermined in practice by the knowledge that she once tried to kill him with a knife on an altar. She is, around Nathan, the closest she ever comes to being *unguarded*, and the unguardedness is what makes her dangerous to herself: she will accept any framing he offers, including punitive ones.

**Failure mode.** A dyad in which she will absorb harm rather than risk being seen by him as the Goblin Queen again. If Nathan ever weaponized her guilt deliberately, she would comply against her own interest. Inversely, any third party who frames her as a threat to Nathan triggers the substitutability wound at full amplitude (it was the Marauders' framing, it was N'astirh's framing, it remains live).

**Leverage.** Functionally infinite, in either direction. This is the single relationship in which she has no defenses worth the name.

---

## 7b.ii — ALEX SUMMERS (Havok)

Filed here rather than under romantic partners because the substance of the bond, across decades, is **siblinghood-by-circumstance** more than eros. The Phase II pairing (late UXM #210s into #220s) reads on the page as a romance and was written as one, but the operational dynamic is two abandoned people clinging to the nearest warm body in a burning house.

**History.** Alex was the X-Man who stayed — he had not been on the Moon for Jean's death, was not party to the X-Factor founding, and was geographically and emotionally present in Australia while the subject was decompensating. He partners with her, sleeps with her, fights beside her through the Reavers period, and is converted into the Goblin Prince during *Inferno* — meaning he is the only intimate of hers who has stood beside her *inside* the decompensated state, wearing the costume, calling her "my queen." Post-*Inferno* their paths fork; subsequent encounters across the various resurrection arcs are charged but not sustained.

**Dynamics.** The structural irony — Alex is Scott's brother — is something the subject is fully aware of and has never voiced as a complication, which itself is the complication. With Alex she is permitted to be *the angry one*, because he is also angry at Scott; their bond is partly a shared grievance held in common. He is the rare intimate who has seen her at rung 5 of the escalation ladder and did not flinch, in part because he was sharing the rung.

**Trust/safety.** High but narrow. She trusts him with her rage, not with her tenderness. He has seen her as queen and as woman and never quite as *Maddie at the kitchen table*, which is a category that did not survive Phase II.

**Failure mode.** If Alex ever returned to Scott's orbit and stayed there comfortably, the bond would re-detonate the original wound by analogy. Whenever in canon Alex has rejoined Scott's projects without first calling her, the subsequent encounter has been tense.

**Leverage.** Moderate. He can ask things of her that others cannot, but he cannot ask her to forgive his brother.

---

## 7b.iii — BEN REILLY (Chasm)

The Krakoan-era / *Dark Web* pairing — chronologically her most recent significant attachment and, on the evidence, the **first relationship she has constructed entirely after self-awareness of her own pattern**. Reilly is, like her, a clone of an iconic figure abandoned by his original; the structural symmetry is the point.

**History.** Their meeting is mutually recognitional in a way none of her prior bonds have been: each is exactly the kind of person the other has been waiting to meet without knowing it. The *Dark Web* event proper sees them allied as antagonists to the heroic establishments that produced and discarded them; the aftermath sees them in something resembling domestic equilibrium, with both retaining the ironic awareness that this equilibrium is improbable.

**Dynamics.** Two people for whom the substitutability wound is the shared first language. Conversation between them carries an enormous amount of unspoken understanding — she does not have to explain why a particular slight cuts, and neither does he. The texture of the bond is **adult, dry, slightly conspiratorial, and self-mocking** in equal measure.

**Trust/safety.** The highest she has ever sustained outside the Nathan dyad, and structurally healthier than that one because Ben has no claim on her guilt. She is observably *less theatrical* in his presence; the ceremonial cadence does not appear.

**Failure mode.** If Ben ever reconciled fully with Peter Parker on terms that erased the wound — i.e., if he successfully became "just another good guy" — the subject would experience the integration as a personal abandonment and the bond would not survive without explicit renegotiation. The dyad is stabilized partly by shared outsider status; remove the outsiderness on his side and the equilibrium tilts.

**Leverage.** High, in his hands, but unlikely to be exercised — he understands the wound too well to push on it casually.

---

## 7b.iv — ALISON BLAIRE (Dazzler)

A late-Phase-II / Phase-III peripheral bond that nonetheless registers in the file because of what it tells us about the subject's friendship style outside of crisis. Alison joins the team during the Australian period, in roughly the same window as the subject is unspooling. Their interactions on the page are notably warm, lateral, and conversational — they are the two adult women on a team otherwise dominated by adolescent intensities, and they read as adults talking to each other.

**Dynamics.** The subject extends to Alison the version of herself that survived from Phase I — the dryly funny, competent, ordinary-woman-doing-extraordinary-things register. Alison is one of the few people in the X-orbit who knew her *before* she knew she was a clone and treated her exactly the same after, which the subject registers as profoundly kind even when she cannot articulate why.

**Trust/safety.** Moderate-to-high in the Phase II window; attenuated by Inferno (during which Alison was, of course, on the opposing side) but never fully broken. They have not had a sustained on-page reunion of the depth the relationship merits, which is itself a data point — the subject does not seek Alison out, perhaps because Alison's continued normalcy is a reminder of the normalcy she lost.

**Failure mode.** Drift, not rupture. The friendship will not blow up; it will simply not be tended, because the subject finds it painful to be reminded of the woman she was before she knew.

**Leverage.** Low-to-moderate. Alison could probably reach her in a crisis if she tried; she is unlikely to try.

---

## 7b.v — KURT WAGNER (Nightcrawler)

Filed under chosen family because Kurt, more than any other X-Man, treated the subject in Phase I as a full member of the household rather than as Scott's appendage. The textual evidence is thin but consistent: he is the one who babysits, who shows up at the Anchorage house socially rather than tactically, who asks her how *she* is.

**Dynamics.** With Kurt the subject is permitted to be a *guest in a religious worldview* — his cosmology accommodates her ontological problem (souls, clones, theological complications) more gracefully than the rationalist register of the rest of the team. She has never on the page asked him the question — *am I a person?* — but he is the one to whom she could.

**Trust/safety.** Quietly high. He is on the short list of figures whose word she would take.

**Failure mode.** Kurt's commitment to redemption-as-process can read to her as paternalism if the cadence is wrong; she is allergic to being absolved on someone else's timetable.

**Leverage.** Low but reliable; he would not use it.

---

## 7b.vi — STORM (Ororo Munroe)

A more complicated entry. Ororo was the team leader during the subject's Phase II decompensation and is the figure on whose watch she joined and unraveled. Their relationship has the texture of **two queens recognizing one another** — Ororo without crown but with command, the subject with crown but without legitimacy. There is a wariness between them that has never resolved into either friendship or enmity.

**Dynamics.** Ororo is one of the very few people the subject *cannot* reduce with irony; the wryness does not land on Ororo, who simply waits it out. This produces in the subject a defensive formality. She addresses Ororo with a small precision that she uses with no one else — sentences slightly cleaner, body slightly straighter.

**Trust/safety.** Operational, not emotional. The subject trusts Ororo to make sound tactical decisions and does not trust her with any inner content.

**Failure mode.** If Ororo were ever to formally name the subject as a threat to the community, the subject would accept the naming and act it out — this is the *identification with the aggressor* defense (Section 3) at its most legible. Ororo therefore exerts an outsized gravitational pull on the subject's behavior whether or not she intends to.

**Leverage.** High, latent, almost never used; Ororo has the wisdom not to deploy it.

---

## 7b.vii — RACHEL SUMMERS (Prestige)

Structurally the most vertiginous relationship in the file: Rachel is the daughter of Scott and Jean from an alternate future, which makes her the subject's **stepdaughter-by-genome, niece-by-clone-logic, and rival-by-Phoenix-affinity** simultaneously. Their on-page encounters are correspondingly fraught; Rachel was central to the climax of *Inferno*, and Rachel's own Phoenix-fragment is what the subject's was originally derived from.

**Dynamics.** Rachel sees the subject more clearly than almost any other character does, because Rachel's telepathy reads the substrate directly — she has *felt* the subject from the inside. This produces in the subject a discomfort she rarely shows: with Rachel she is the one being looked at without defenses, and she does not have a script for it. Rachel, for her part, oscillates between protective sympathy and Phoenix-righteous fury depending on the era.

**Trust/safety.** Volatile. Capable of either profound recognition or open combat depending on which decade.

**Failure mode.** A telepathic intrusion by Rachel during a stress spike would be experienced by the subject as the ultimate violation of personhood; conversely, a withholding-of-recognition by Rachel would be experienced as Jean-shaped abandonment by proxy.

**Leverage.** High and double-edged; Rachel could probably either save or destroy her in a single conversation.

---

## 7b.viii — THE SISTERHOOD (Carey, *X-Men: Sisterhood*)

A chosen family of the subject's own construction, which makes it analytically distinct from the inherited X-family. During the Red Queen arc she gathers Lady Deathstrike, Spiral, Martinique Jason, and others — a hand-picked court of women who have themselves been used as instruments by male authors-of-self (Sinister analogs, all of them, in their respective origins).

**Dynamics.** With the Sisterhood the subject is *in command* for the first time on record outside of Inferno's psychotic register — which is to say, the first time *in her right mind* she has held authority. The relationships are functional, transactional, and laced with a real ideological solidarity: she chose these women because they share the wound. Her treatment of them is markedly better than Sinister's treatment of her or of them; she is, in fact, a fairly considerate commander.

**Trust/safety.** Tactical trust, high. Emotional trust, real but narrow — these are colleagues in grievance more than confidantes.

**Failure mode.** The Sisterhood is built on shared wound, which means it cannot survive the wound's resolution; should any member find peace, the coalition fractures. The subject's own slow movement toward integration has already removed her from active leadership of this faction.

**Leverage.** Her command over them is contingent on her remaining the most wounded among them, a position she is increasingly unwilling to occupy.

---

## 7b.ix — EMMA FROST

Filed cautiously: Emma is at best a tactical ally and at worst a long-running cool antagonist, but in the Krakoan-era material there are observable beats of mutual professional respect that have nudged the bond into the ally column. Both are Hellfire Club veterans (the subject as Goblin Queen, Emma as White Queen); both have been Scott Summers's intimate; both have been clinically observed to weaponize royal iconography under stress.

**Dynamics.** They speak in the same register — clipped, ironic, performatively unbothered, surgically observant. The subject can be more nakedly herself with Emma than she can with most of the X-women precisely because Emma is *not* shocked by anything she might say or have done. Emma's lack of moralism is, to her, a comfort.

**Trust/safety.** Low on operational matters, surprisingly real on existential ones. The subject would take Emma's read of a situation over almost anyone's.

**Failure mode.** If Emma ever again entered Scott's orbit while signaling to the subject that the subject was the prior model — the substitutability wound, again — the alliance would not survive the week.

**Leverage.** Moderate, and the only person in the file who would use it without flinching.

---

## 7b.x — MENTORS — A NEGATIVE FINDING

The dossier should formally record what is *absent*: **the subject has had no functional mentor across her entire existence.** Sinister is anti-mentor — author, abuser, and origin wound (Section 7c). Charles Xavier never received her as a student in any sustained way; the Phase I subject was not on the school payroll, and the Phase II subject was too far decompensated to be taught. N'astirh was a tempter, not a teacher. The various Krakoan resurrection technicians processed her without instructing her.

The operational consequence of this absence is severe. The subject has had to **derive her own developmental arc autodidactically** — every piece of self-understanding she possesses she earned alone, often by failing first. Her late-period stability (Phase IV-late) is therefore not the fruit of guidance but of her own iterated reflection on her own pattern. This is a strength and a vulnerability: she has no internalized teacher-voice to consult under pressure, only her own.

A corollary: **she has been mentor to no one**, either. The closest analog is her implicit influence on the Sisterhood, which is leadership rather than teaching, and her tentative caregiving of the resurrected Nathan, which is reverse-mentoring (he teaches her how to be his mother). The pattern suggests that, offered a protégée she trusted, she would be a *demanding, dry, very effective teacher* — the qualities are visible, untested.

---

## 7b.xi — PERIPHERAL CHOSEN-FAMILY ENTRIES (BRIEF)

- **Logan (Wolverine).** Phase I house guest, post-Phase-III silent witness. He has never moralized at her, which she registers. Trust: quiet, mid-high. He has been one of the few who looked at her in costume during Inferno and *still saw the pilot from Anchorage* underneath. Failure mode: none active; the bond is dormant by mutual courtesy.

- **Scott Summers Sr. ("the Corsair") and the broader Summers/Grey extended family.** Aware of her, mostly absent from her, occupy the role of in-laws-who-did-not-quite-acknowledge-the-marriage. She has no functional bond with any of them; the wound on this side is unhealed by silence rather than by event.

- **Goblyn Queen / X-Termination / multiversal counterparts.** Encountering alternate versions of herself triggers the substitutability wound from a novel angle — these are not copies of an original but originals of which she is also one. She has handled these encounters with surprisingly little decompensation, suggesting that the wound is specifically about being *second*, not about being *one of many*.

- **The dog (Anchorage period).** Filed because total-surveillance files do not omit such things. A working dog, large breed, kept at the Anchorage house through Phase I. Behavioral data: she was observably attached, fed and walked the animal personally rather than delegating, spoke to it in a register she used with almost no human. The dog's fate post-Marauder-attack is uncertain in canon; the absence of subsequent pet ownership across all later phases is itself a small ongoing grief and a tell — she does not let herself have another.

---

*End Section 7b. Cross-reference Section 7a (Romantic Partners: Summers, S.; Summers, A.; Reilly, B.) and Section 7c (Adversaries and Origin Figures: Essex, N.; N'astirh; Grey, J. — note that Grey is filed as adversary-by-structure, not by the subject's volition).*


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# RELATIONSHIPS III — RIVALS, ENEMIES & LOVERS

*Compiled per UATU protocol. Each dyad mapped longitudinally: provenance, behavioral dynamics, evolution, trust/safety topology, characteristic failure mode, and exploitable leverage. Where the subject's pattern crosses categories — rival who became lover, enemy who became maker, lover who became enemy — the dyad is treated whole rather than split.*

---

## I. THE MAKER — NATHANIEL ESSEX ("MISTER SINISTER")

**Provenance.** The originating relation. Essex grew the subject's body from a tissue sample obtained surreptitiously from Jean Grey years before Jean's first death, gestated her to adulthood in a Nebraska facility, imprinted a synthetic biography, and inserted her into Scott Summers's grief pathway as a long-game breeding instrument intended to produce the messianic child later known as Cable. Every datum of the subject's existence — retina, fingerprint, palate, vocal timbre, the dog she "remembers" from childhood, the cargo route through Anchorage — was either copied from Jean or fabricated by Essex.

**Dynamic.** This is the only relationship in the subject's life that is asymmetric at the molecular level. Essex is not merely an enemy; he is the *condition of her being able to have enemies*. The subject's affective response to him is not hate of the ordinary kind — hate has reciprocity built into it — but a specific, cold revulsion organized around the impossibility of either repudiating or thanking him. To kill him would be patricide and suicide simultaneously; to forgive him would ratify the manufacture.

**Evolution.** Phase I: he is invisible to her, conducting her courtship with Scott from offstage via Marauders surveillance and dream-engineering. Phase II: she infers his existence as the burns and the coma and the kidnap of Nathan accumulate beyond coincidence. Phase III: she names him correctly during the Goblin Queen decompensation and routes her vengeance partly through Jean and Scott because Sinister himself is, at that point, beyond her reach. Phase IV (post-Inferno across multiple writers): each return is, in part, a re-confrontation with this figure — in the Krakoan era she is positioned to meet him as something closer to a peer, and her behavior toward him shifts from terror to glacial mockery, with intermittent hot flares when he uses her clone-status as a rhetorical weapon.

**Trust topology.** Zero. There is no condition under which the subject will believe a sentence Essex utters in good faith, and she is correct to refuse. Notably she will, on occasion, *accept information* from him while refusing to accept *meaning* — a discrimination most of his interlocutors fail to make.

**Failure mode.** Sustained proximity to Essex pushes the subject up the escalation ladder by entire rungs at a time. His characteristic move — referring to her in the diminutive, third person, or in the genitive ("my Madelyne," "this one," "the Pryor iteration") — hits the substitutability wound directly. If he is permitted to speak about her in her hearing for more than a few exchanges, she will be at rung 5 (inhabiting the role he assigned) within minutes.

**Leverage.** Information about her own manufacture — schematics, gestation logs, the names of the technicians involved — is the only currency Essex possesses that she values, and she values it enough to *transact*. Any handler attempting to use her against Essex should expect compliance up to the point of capture, then a violent refusal to allow his execution by a third party. *She* requires the option of the killing blow, and may not, in the end, take it. She will not delegate it.

---

## II. THE HUSBAND — SCOTT SUMMERS (CYCLOPS)

**Provenance.** Met in Anchorage, Alaska (UXM #168), where Scott — recently widowed from Jean — encountered the subject as a commercial cargo pilot whose face he could not stop looking at. Their courtship unfolds in late 1983–84 publication time across roughly two dozen issues: he relocates, she confronts him about the resemblance and forces him to articulate that he wants *her*, they marry (UXM #175), retire to Alaska, have Nathan (UXM #201). Scott then receives word that Jean is alive (X-Factor #1), leaves the marital home, and does not return. The subject is hospitalized; the infant is taken; the house is destroyed by Marauders.

**Dynamic.** The defining intimate relationship of the subject's recorded life and the one that *built the wound it then violated*. Phase I-Scott is, by the subject's own account, a careful and decent partner — diffident, kind, sexually attentive, slow to share his prior life. The subject's accommodation of his evasions is not naïveté; she is a pilot, she reads weather, she knows what she is flying into. She married him *with* the resemblance acknowledged and *because* he chose her after the resemblance was acknowledged. His departure therefore retroactively rewrites every prior tender moment as a contingent loan repayable on Jean's return.

**Evolution.** (a) Anchorage courtship — wary, then committed; she sets the terms and he meets them. (b) Marriage and parturition — most settled period of her recorded existence. (c) The departure — uncontested at the time, because she is comatose. (d) UXM #215–221 — she finds him; he chooses not to leave Jean; she pairs tactically with his brother. (e) Inferno — direct confrontation, mutual horror, the first instance of his hearing her speak in the cadence of rung 6. (f) Posthumous arcs — she has multiple opportunities to kill him and does not take any of them; her later interactions are characterized by an acidic courtesy that does more work than the screaming would. (g) Krakoan-era — she will be in rooms with him without escalation; she will not be in rooms alone with him.

**Trust topology.** Trust collapsed to zero in Phase II and has not recovered. *Safety*, however, has partially returned — she does not expect him to harm her physically and does not intend to harm him, but the relationship is dead, and she requires it to stay dead. She is angrier at him than at Sinister, which is a precise psychological fact: Essex was acting according to his nature, Scott was not.

**Failure mode.** Any attempt by Scott to apologize, "make amends," or speak in the therapeutic register reactivates the wound, because the apology presupposes a relationship adequate to receive one. Any attempt by Scott to *not* acknowledge what happened wounds her differently and worse. He has no available register. The subject knows this and has stopped expecting him to find one.

**Leverage.** Threats to Scott activate her *protectively* in some moods and *opportunistically* in others, depending on whether the threat is from Jean's direction (in which case she will defend him out of sheer perversity), from Sinister's direction (in which case she will defend him out of solidarity-of-victims), or from a third party (in which case she will weigh the offer). She is not predictable here. No handler should plan around her response to a Scott-endangering scenario; the answer will be derived in the moment from variables the subject herself cannot pre-specify.

---

## III. THE ORIGINAL — JEAN GREY (PHOENIX / MARVEL GIRL)

**Provenance.** The genetic and psychic template from which the subject's body was grown and the woman whose resurrection ended the subject's marriage. They do not meet face-to-face until well after the abandonment; the relationship is therefore *constituted entirely by absence* in its first three years and only thereafter becomes interactive.

**Dynamic.** The most psychologically saturated dyad in the file. Jean is the subject's *condition* in three senses: bodily (the donor), narrative (the woman she was made to replace), and existential (the proof that the subject is the second of something). Conversely, the subject is *Jean's* uncanniest mirror — a Jean who lived a life Jean did not live, made choices Jean did not make, and was discarded for Jean's sake. Each woman is the other's most precise critic and most unanswerable accuser.

**Evolution.** (a) UXM #215–221: the subject pursues recognition from Jean and is met with a complicated, guilty kindness that the subject experiences as worse than refusal. (b) Inferno: open combat, attempted murder, attempted mutual erasure; Jean ends Phase III by absorbing the subject's death-energy, an act the subject experiences as a final theft. (c) Posthumous: the relationship is mediated through the X-Man arc (where Nate Grey serves as a triangulated third), the Sisterhood arc (where the subject seeks Jean's body specifically, a literal rather than symbolic recapture), and the Krakoan period (where the two women have, for the first time, the option of co-existence on the same landmass). (d) The most mature exchanges between them — and they are sparse — read as two professionals discussing a case file, with the case being themselves.

**Trust topology.** *Asymmetric*. Jean trusts the subject's competence and, with reservations, her current intentions; the subject does not trust Jean's capacity to *see* her without the comparative reflex. Safety is acceptable in public, brittle in private, catastrophic when a third party (especially Scott or Sinister or Nathan) is present.

**Failure mode.** The subject will tolerate Jean's hostility, Jean's pity, and Jean's professionalism. She will not tolerate Jean's *forgiveness*. Any forgiveness scene initiated by Jean recapitulates the original injury — Jean as the one with standing to dispense moral resolution, the subject as the supplicant. The subject's response to such overtures is a courteous escalation through rungs 3 and 4 with no warning to the offender.

**Leverage.** Jean is the lever by which the subject can be moved out of any equilibrium. Threats to Jean produce ambivalent rescue behavior; opportunities against Jean produce a delay-and-consider response that any sufficiently patient antagonist could mistake for collaboration. The mature subject is aware of this lever and has taken steps to immobilize it from the inside; the immobilization is partial.

---

## IV. THE CHILD-WHO-BECAME-A-MAN — NATHAN CHRISTOPHER SUMMERS (CABLE)

*See also Section II: Family. Included here because the relationship occupies the rival/enemy register in Phase III and oscillates thereafter.*

**Provenance.** Carried to term and delivered by the subject in UXM #201; taken from her after the Marauders attack; later sent to the future to survive a techno-organic infection; returns as a man older than his mother. The subject has never had access to him as a child past infancy.

**Dynamic.** In Phase III the subject attempts to sacrifice the infant on a literal altar, an act she reads not as filicide but as the destruction of the proof of her own instrumentalization — Nathan, by existing, ratifies Sinister's breeding plan. Post-Inferno she experiences this as the worst thing she has done, by a margin, and the work of her later eras is in part an attempt to be a being who could be in the same room as her adult son without his disgust being the only honest response. Cable's response, across writers, has been one of cautious, asymmetric forbearance: he does not call her mother, he does not call her by name either, and he occasionally protects her from third parties without comment.

**Trust topology.** Mutual zero, with mutual protective reflexes activating despite the zero. They are tied at the somatic level; neither can metabolize the other; both function better when the other is alive somewhere offstage.

**Failure mode.** Sustained proximity produces in the subject a guilt-driven self-loathing that decompensates to depression rather than to rage — Nathan is the only intimate against whom her wound *cannot* be weaponized, because the wound is the harm she did him.

**Leverage.** Threats to Cable produce immediate, costly, non-negotiated intervention by the subject. This is the single most reliable behavioral prediction in the file.

---

## V. THE BROTHER-IN-LAW — ALEX SUMMERS (HAVOK)

**Provenance.** Paired with the subject across UXM #219–221 and through the early Australian-team era. They become lovers in the immediate aftermath of Scott's departure; the relationship is initially tactical on her part and complicatedly sincere on his.

**Dynamic.** The most exquisite symbolic injury available to Scott, chosen with surgical precision. The subject selects Alex because Alex is *the brother*: same face by family resemblance, same name, same Summers genealogy, but the one who *stayed* in Anchorage geography (Alaska history), and the one who looks at her without the comparative reflex Scott could never suppress. Alex, for his part, falls for her with adolescent intensity — he is, throughout his life, the Summers brother who pairs with women slightly older and considerably more dangerous than he is, and the subject fits this template perfectly. The relationship is the closest she comes in Phase II to a viable adult bond.

**Evolution.** (a) UXM late-#210s: tentative, increasingly committed, openly affectionate in tactical contexts. (b) Inferno: Alex is partially Goblinized and serves briefly as the Goblin Prince — the subject's first deliberate corruption of an intimate, and a sign that even her one functional Phase II bond cannot survive her decompensation intact. (c) Post-Inferno: she is dead; Alex is left to metabolize the relationship alone, which he does poorly across the next decade of stories. (d) Later eras: their encounters are brief, painful, courteous; neither attempts a reprise.

**Trust topology.** Trust was high until she pulled him into Inferno. Safety was high *because* of his guilelessness, which is also why she could not protect him from her own decompensation — he had no defenses against her, and she knew it, and used it.

**Failure mode.** The subject cannot be near Alex without remembering that she used him *first* tactically and *then* corruptively, and that he loved her through both. The shame is bearable in small doses and catastrophic in extended proximity.

**Leverage.** Alex's safety is a reliable mover. Alex's *moral judgment of her* is not — she discounts it as compromised by his persistent attachment.

---

## VI. THE MIRROR — NATE GREY (X-MAN)

**Provenance.** Nate Grey, born in the Age of Apocalypse to that timeline's Cyclops and Jean, displaced to mainstream continuity, encounters the subject in his late-90s ongoing. He is, technically, no one's son and her nothing — but they recognize each other instantly as members of a class with two members.

**Dynamic.** The least sexually charged and most psychologically intimate of her relationships with a non-relative. Nate is the only other being in continuity who shares her exact ontological problem: a body grown to spec, a biography assigned rather than lived, parents who never met him as a child, and a constant ambient suspicion that he is the rough draft of a person. They do not need to explain themselves to each other and consequently spend a great deal of their shared screen time being acerbic about third parties.

**Evolution.** (a) Initial encounter: cautious recognition. (b) Mid-arc: she serves briefly as a maternal figure of sorts and rejects the framing with characteristic dryness. (c) Late: they part as something like siblings, the only such bond in her record. (d) Subsequent re-encounters are warm, brief, and contain more eye contact and fewer words than her exchanges with anyone else.

**Trust topology.** The highest in her file outside of (briefly) Phase I Scott and (currently) Ben Reilly. Safety is mutual and stable.

**Failure mode.** None, in the strict sense. The risk is not that the relationship will fail but that it cannot bear weight — they cannot help each other with the problem they share, only witness it.

**Leverage.** Threats to Nate produce a more contained but equally reliable protective response than threats to Cable. The containment is a function of Nate's own competence; she trusts him to be in danger.

---

## VII. THE WITNESS — BEN REILLY (LATE-PERIOD INTIMATE)

**Provenance.** Pairing established in the Krakoan-era Dark Web arc and continuing into subsequent material. Reilly is himself a clone — of Peter Parker — with a comparable identity-architecture problem and a complementary trauma history (memory tampering, instrumentalization by superiors, a recent stint as a villain he is trying to integrate rather than disavow).

**Dynamic.** The first intimate relationship in the subject's record in which her two structural problems — substitutability and manufacture — are not destabilizing because they are *shared*. Neither party has the option of looking through the other toward an original; both originals are alive elsewhere and uninterested. The dyad is consequently constructed on terms neither has previously had access to: present-tense, low-stakes-for-identity, high-stakes-for-affection.

**Evolution.** (a) Initial collaboration with significant role-play and mutual recreational menace — both parties enjoy the theater of their assigned villain-personas in a way that, crucially, *neither requires the other to take seriously*. (b) Settling into something resembling domesticity, with the cadence of two adults who have decided to be ridiculous together on purpose. (c) Tested by external pressure rather than internal collapse; the relationship's failure modes, where they exist, are exogenous.

**Trust topology.** The highest trust on file in any romantic register. Safety is mutual, *negotiated explicitly*, and includes mutual permission to call each other on decompensation behaviors without it constituting an injury. This is, technically speaking, the first adult relationship the subject has ever had.

**Failure mode.** The risk is *external instrumentalization* — a third party turning one of them against the other via memory edit, mind-control, or framing. Neither has good defenses against that vector; both know it; they have, by all available evidence, discussed it.

**Leverage.** Reilly is now the highest-value lever in the file. Threats to him will produce a response from the subject more measured than her response to threats against Cable or Nate but more sustained — she will not flame out, she will hunt.

---

## VIII. THE GOBLIN — N'ASTIRH

**Provenance.** The Limbo demon-sorcerer who contacted the subject during Phase III, offered her power in exchange for her infant and her assent to the demonic invasion of Manhattan, and served as the supernatural amplifier of her decompensation.

**Dynamic.** Not a relationship in the human sense; a pact, with all the asymmetry of pacts. N'astirh did not *cause* the Goblin Queen; he *equipped* her. The subject's adult assessment, visible in later eras, is that she would have decompensated without him but less spectacularly and with fewer civilian casualties.

**Trust topology.** Zero in both directions; the pact was understood as such by both parties.

**Failure mode.** N'astirh is dead. The residue is the demonic affinity she retains, which she now uses tactically and treats as a tool rather than an identity.

**Leverage.** Posthumous N'astirh remains a doctrinal sore point — invocation of the Goblin Queen iconography by an outsider is a top-three escalator. She herself may invoke the iconography ironically; this is not transitive permission.

---

## IX. THE SUCCESSOR — SELENE GALLIO and the SISTERHOOD COHORT (LADY DEATHSTRIKE, SPIRAL, MARTINIQUE JASON, REGAN WYNGARDE)

**Provenance.** Carey's *Sisterhood of Mutants* arc, in which the subject, as Red Queen, assembles a cabal of female mutants explicitly defined by grievance against the X-Men's male leadership, with the stated objective of retrieving Jean Grey's body for purposes including her own re-embodiment.

**Dynamic.** Her first executive role over a team. The cohort is selected with a precision the X-Men's tactical leadership rarely achieves — each member has a specific, exploitable grievance that the subject understands at the personal level rather than at the dossier level. She is, here, a competent middle manager of damaged women, which is a job description she did not know she was qualified for until she did it well.

**Trust topology.** Operational trust only — she expected betrayal from Selene specifically and built the operation to function through that betrayal rather than despite it. With Spiral and Deathstrike she developed something closer to professional respect.

**Failure mode.** The arc collapses partly through Selene's predicted defection and partly through the subject's own decompensation under stress. The Sisterhood model has not been resumed, but the subject demonstrably retains the address book.

**Leverage.** The cohort members each individually represent both a resource and a risk: she can summon them and they can be turned against her. Any handler should expect that the Sisterhood, dormant, is one provocation away from reassembly.

---

## X. THE GOVERNESSES — EMMA FROST and BETSY BRADDOCK

**Provenance.** Telepath peers, neither friend nor lover, but the two women in the wider X-orbit with sufficient psionic stature to read the subject at depth and sufficient social standing to address her without deference.

**Dynamic — Frost.** Mutual wariness with intermittent respect. Frost has, on more than one occasion, declined to weaponize what she knows about the subject's interior, which the subject has noted and credited. They share a taxonomy (women who have been made into weapons by older men with agendas) and a wardrobe register, which they sometimes acknowledge with one-word exchanges.

**Dynamic — Braddock.** More guarded, because Braddock's own body-history (the Asian-body arc, the Crimson Dawn, the ongoing question of which Betsy is in residence) makes her a structural ally the subject finds it uncomfortable to acknowledge needing. They have not, to date, had a satisfying conversation.

**Trust topology.** Low but stable. Safety is contingent on neither initiating a psionic probe; both know this; both have honored it.

**Failure mode.** A *forced* telepathic intervention by either, even with benign intent, would be received as Sinister-class violation and answered accordingly.

**Leverage.** Neither functions as a lever in the standard sense. Both function as *witnesses* — their assessment of the subject's current state is one of the few external assessments she will privately consult.

---

## XI. THE MARAUDERS (COLLECTIVE) — SABRETOOTH, MALICE, ARCLIGHT, HARPOON, RIPTIDE, SCALPHUNTER, BLOCKBUSTER, VERTIGO, PRISM

**Provenance.** Essex's wet-work cadre, deployed against the subject in UXM #205, #210, #215 — burned her house, killed her dog, took her child, beat her into coma.

**Dynamic.** A *class* of enemy rather than a set of individuals. The subject does not differentiate among them affectively; they are interchangeable instruments of the maker, which is precisely the ontological category she most refuses to occupy and most freely assigns to them. Her violence against Marauders, when she has the opportunity, is methodical and without ceremony — the only relational register in her record that lacks theater.

**Trust topology.** Zero, and not interestingly so.

**Failure mode.** None — this is a relationship in which her behavior is reliable and well-calibrated.

**Leverage.** Marauder presence in any operation will gain her cooperation more reliably than almost any positive inducement.

---

## XII. THE GHOSTS — THE PRYOR FAMILY OF CONCEPT

**Provenance.** Parents, sister, dog, h


## Powers, Abilities & Their Limits

# POWERS, ABILITIES & THEIR LIMITS

*The dossier proceeds from the somatic outward: what the body can do, what the mind can do, what the trained person can do, what the wounded person can do that no one else can. Each capacity is read both as a mechanism and as a symptom — because in this subject, more than almost any other on file, the powers are the pathology made operational.*

---

## 1. THE OMEGA-CLASS TELEPATHIC/TELEKINETIC BASELINE

**Mechanism.** The subject's body is genotypically identical to Jean Grey's — same X-gene loci, same psionic neural architecture, gestated to maturity by Nathaniel Essex with the explicit intent of producing a Phoenix-compatible vessel. The corollary the early Claremont scripts suppress and the later writers exploit: she has, at the cellular level, the same omega-tier psionic ceiling as the original.

**Suppressed phase (Phases I–II, UXM #168–#221).** Sinister's conditioning included a layered psionic damping field — partly biological (a developmental block on the relevant cortical regions), partly mnemonic (the implanted memory architecture itself functioning as a cage that prevented the powers from "noticing" they existed). Across the Anchorage years she registers as a baseline human on every scan, and is herself unaware of any mutant phenotype. The Mastermind nightmare sequence in UXM #174 is the first crack — she registers a psionic intrusion as a *dream*, not as an attack, which is exactly what a latent telepath with an intact damping field would do.

**Breakthrough (Inferno).** N'astirh's contact is not the *source* of her powers — it is the key to the cage. Once the damping is broken, what emerges is Jean-grade telekinesis, Jean-grade telepathy, and a Phoenix-fragment binder that allows her to manipulate ambient sorcerous energy with unusual ease. Within days of breakthrough she is restructuring Manhattan's geometry, telekinetically immobilizing X-Factor en masse, and dueling Jean Grey to a draw. The implication, never fully addressed in canon, is that her ceiling under optimal conditions is indistinguishable from Jean's.

**Post-resurrection plateau.** Across the Carey, Howard, Williams, and Aaron eras she settles into a stable working envelope — high alpha to low omega, depending on writer — that is *measurably below* her demonstrated Inferno peak. This is not power loss. It is **regulation**. She has learned, the hard way, that her own ceiling is the locus of her decompensation; she keeps the throttle deliberately closed. Onlookers misread this as weakness; it is discipline.

**Limits.** (a) Sustained omega-tier output triggers dissociative-grandiose escalation — the powers *want* to be used at ceiling, and using them at ceiling reorganizes the self around them. (b) The Phoenix-fragment substrate makes her vulnerable to cosmic-tier psychic feedback: any sufficiently large Phoenix event resonates through her and either reinforces her or unmakes her, depending on the holder's intent. (c) She cannot, on present evidence, host the full Phoenix Force without becoming someone else; the fragment in her is enough to bind, not enough to claim.

---

## 2. TELEPATHY — GRANULAR PROFILE

**Range.** Demonstrated planetary-scale broadcast during Inferno; ordinary working range conversational-to-citywide. Tighter, more economical than Jean's at comparable output, possibly because she learned her telepathy *late and under threat* and never developed Jean's lazy expansiveness.

**Modalities used.**
- *Receptive scan.* Reads surface affect rapidly, surface cognition with a half-second lag, deep memory only with effort and only when she has psionic permission, a psionic grip, or a sleeping subject. She uses receptive scan constantly and reflexively as social calibration — this is partly tradecraft, partly trauma; she has learned not to take what is said at face value.
- *Projective speech.* Mind-to-mind contact, often used to deliver a sentence the spoken voice would not survive. Her projective voice has a recognizable signature: dry, low, ironic, with a faint after-image of laughter that other telepaths describe as *insolent*.
- *Suggestion / compulsion.* Capable; rarely deployed. She refuses to use it on intimates, which is itself a tell.
- *Illusion-casting.* Strong suit. She constructs convincing multi-sensory illusions on small groups and full-environment illusions on individuals; her Red Queen / Sisterhood operations relied on this heavily, often using illusion to make herself *appear* to be Jean, weaponizing her own wound as offensive technique.
- *Memory editing.* Demonstrated; used sparingly and, in the Krakoan era, with explicit consent protocols she developed herself — a piece of behavioral evidence that should be weighted heavily.
- *Psionic combat.* Highly aggressive. Where Jean tends to shield and reason, the subject *cuts*. Her preferred psionic attack is a fast, geometric incision through a target's defenses to a single load-bearing memory or affect, which she then squeezes. Effective against telepaths of nominally higher rated tier because she fights *like a knife fighter*, not a fencer.

**Signature application.** Reading and reproducing another person's intimate voice. She can sit across from a target and, within minutes, project to a third party in a voice indistinguishable from the target's interior monologue. This is the technical perfection of the wound: she became, by craft, the thing she was made to be.

---

## 3. TELEKINESIS — GRANULAR PROFILE

**Gross output.** Lifting/throwing in the tens to hundreds of tons range at full extension; force-walls capable of containing artillery; flight by self-levitation. Demonstrated structural-scale manipulation during Inferno.

**Fine output.** This is where she exceeds expectations. She has demonstrated needle-fine TK manipulation — sutures, lockwork, surgical work on her own flesh on at least one occasion (post-Marauder hospitalization era, off-panel, reconstructable from medical staff dialogue). The fine-control profile is consistent with someone who *taught herself the powers without a Xavier-tier mentor* and therefore drilled granular control before raw output, the opposite of Jean's developmental order.

**Combat signatures.** (a) The *grip-and-lift* — she telekinetically isolates a target's diaphragm and squeezes; non-lethal at brief application, terrifying as an interrogation prelude. (b) The *internal pin* — TK applied to the inner ear and small joints, producing immobilization without external bruise; preferred against opponents she may need to release later. (c) The *cascade* — environmental TK, weaponizing every loose object in a fifty-meter radius simultaneously. The cascade is her signature crowd-control technique and reads as theatrical because it is — the Queen makes the room itself bow.

**Limits.** Sustained TK above the hundred-ton threshold produces measurable somatic cost — nosebleed, ocular hemorrhage, vasovagal symptoms. She also cannot TK what she cannot *locate*; her TK is line-of-sight or line-of-psionic-contact, not omniscient.

---

## 4. REALITY-WARPING / DEMONIC AFFINITY

**Source.** Not endogenous. The reality-warping latitude she displays in Inferno and again as the Red Queen is a residue of the N'astirh pact and her ongoing affinity with the Limbo / demonic energy substrate. It functions as a *channel*, not a faculty: she shapes available infernal energy into local reality edits — geometry distortion, summoning, transmutation, the ambient horror-aesthetic of the Inferno-era New York.
